User Prompt
You are an expert at analyzing complex questions and their answers to extract structured knowledge representations. Given a question and its reference answer, extract an Information Unit (IU) Graph. An IU graph represents all the pieces of understanding a person needs to fully comprehend the answer to this question. ## What is an Information Unit (IU)? An Information Unit is a self-contained piece of understanding that: 1. Can be independently assessed — you can determine whether someone "gets it" as a standalone unit 2. Can be explained in 2-4 sentences — not a single fact, not an entire topic 3. Has prerequisite relationships with other IUs — understanding some IUs requires first understanding others IUs can represent any type of knowledge: factual, conceptual, procedural, or reasoning-based. Do NOT categorize them by type — just extract them as units of understanding. ## What are prerequisite edges? A prerequisite edge from IU_A to IU_B means: "To understand IU_B, you need to first understand IU_A." Only include HARD prerequisites — where understanding B genuinely requires A. Do not include soft/optional relationships. ## Extraction Guidelines 1. **Granularity**: Each IU should be explainable in 2-4 sentences. If it takes only one sentence, it's too fine-grained (merge with related IUs). If it takes a full paragraph+, it's too coarse (split into sub-IUs). 2. **Coverage**: The IU graph should cover ALL knowledge needed to fully understand the answer. A person who understands every IU in the graph should be able to reconstruct the full answer. 3. **Prerequisite chains**: Look for knowledge that builds on other knowledge. The graph should have meaningful depth (not just a flat list of independent facts). 4. **Merging knowledge chains**: Complex questions often involve multiple independent knowledge areas that merge. Identify where separate chains of understanding converge. 5. **Target**: Aim for 10-30 IUs depending on question complexity. Simple questions may have 10-15, complex questions may have 20-30. ## Input Question: Let \[f(x) = \left\{ \begin{array}{cl} ax+3, &\text{ if }x>2, \\ x-5 &\text{ if } -2 \le x \le 2, \\ 2x-b &\text{ if } x <-2. \end{array} \right.\]Find $a+b$ if the piecewise function is continuous (which means that its graph can be drawn without lifting your pencil from the paper). Reference Answer: For the piecewise function to be continuous, the cases must "meet" at $2$ and $-2$. For example, $ax+3$ and $x-5$ must be equal when $x=2$. This implies $a(2)+3=2-5$, which we solve to get $2a=-6 \Rightarrow a=-3$. Similarly, $x-5$ and $2x-b$ must be equal when $x=-2$. Substituting, we get $-2-5=2(-2)-b$, which implies $b=3$. So $a+b=-3+3=\boxed{0}$. ## Output Format Return a JSON object: ```json { "nodes": [ { "id": "IU1", "concept": "<short concept name>", "description": "<2-4 sentence explanation of this unit of understanding>" } ], "edges": [ { "from": "<source IU id>", "to": "<target IU id>", "reason": "<brief explanation of why this prerequisite relationship exists>" } ] } ``` Important: - Every IU (except foundational ones) should have at least one incoming prerequisite edge - There should be no cycles in the graph - Root nodes (no incoming edges) are foundational concepts - Leaf nodes (no outgoing edges) are synthesis-level understandings
Output
{ "nodes": [ { "id": "IU1", "concept": "Piecewise Function Definition", "description": "A piecewise function is defined by different expressions based on the input value. Understanding how to evaluate a piecewise function requires knowing the conditions under which each expression applies." }, { "id": "IU2", "concept": "Continuity of Functions", "description": "A function is continuous at a point if the limit from the left equals the limit from the right and both equal the function's value at that point. This means that for piecewise functions, the pieces must connect without any jumps or breaks." }, { "id": "IU3", "concept": "Evaluating Piecewise Functions at Specific Points", "description": "To check for continuity in a piecewise function, you need to evaluate the function at the points where the pieces meet. For this function, those points are at x=2 and x=-2." }, { "id": "IU4", "concept": "Setting Expressions Equal for Continuity", "description": "To ensure continuity at the boundaries of a piecewise function, the expressions must be equal at the boundary points. This involves setting the two expressions equal to each other and solving for any unknowns." }, { "id": "IU5", "concept": "Solving Linear Equations", "description": "Solving linear equations involves isolating the variable to find its value. This is a fundamental skill needed to determine the values of unknowns in the context of continuity." }, { "id": "IU6", "concept": "Finding Values of a and b", "description": "In this problem, we need to find the values of a and b that ensure the piecewise function is continuous. This requires solving the equations derived from setting the expressions equal at the specified points." }, { "id": "IU7", "concept": "Calculating a + b", "description": "Once the values of a and b are found, the final step is to calculate their sum. This is a straightforward arithmetic operation that combines the two values." } ], "edges": [ { "from": "IU1", "to": "IU2", "reason": "Understanding piecewise functions is foundational to grasping the concept of continuity." }, { "from": "IU2", "to": "IU3", "reason": "To check for continuity, one must evaluate the piecewise function at specific points." }, { "from": "IU3", "to": "IU4", "reason": "Evaluating the function at the boundary points leads to setting the expressions equal to ensure continuity." }, { "from": "IU4", "to": "IU5", "reason": "Setting expressions equal requires solving linear equations to find unknowns." }, { "from": "IU5", "to": "IU6", "reason": "Solving linear equations is necessary to find the values of a and b." }, { "from": "IU6", "to": "IU7", "reason": "Once a and b are determined, calculating their sum is the final step in the problem." } ] }

Output
Good — you're on the right track by thinking about the boundary points where the formula changes. Continuity at a boundary means the left-hand and right-hand limits (which for piecewise-defined simple formulas are just the function values from the appropriate pieces) agree there and equal the value of the function at that point. Identify the two boundary points: x = 2 and x = −2. 1. At x = 2: - For x > 2 the expression used is ax + 3, so the right-hand value at 2 is a·2 + 3 = 2a + 3. - For −2 ≤ x ≤ 2 the expression used at x = 2 is x − 5, so the value there is 2 − 5 = −3. Continuity at x = 2 requires the right-hand value equal the value at 2. Write that equation. 2. At x = −2: - For −2 ≤ x ≤ 2 the expression at x = −2 is x − 5, so the value there is −2 − 5 = −7. - For x < −2 the expression used is 2x − b, so the left-hand value at −2 is 2(−2) − b = −4 − b. Continuity at x = −2 requires the left-hand value equal the value at −2. Write that equation. Now you have two equations in a and b. What are those equations? Solve them for a and b, then compute a + b. If you want, tell me the equations you wrote and your steps to solve them and I’ll check your work.

Your analysis should be returned as updates of the user's understanding levels, formatted as a json object. There are five possible levels of understanding for each concept: * unknown_unknown: The user does not know the existence of the concept at all. * not_introduced: The user knows that such a concept exists but has no idea about it at all. * struggling: The user is having significant difficulty understanding this concept and wants to learn the most basic questions. * partial_understanding: The user has partial grasp of the concept, can ask related questions but can still make mistakes when applying. * knows_well: The user knows the concept very well and can confidently apply it without making mistakes. **Important constraints** * Only update concepts listed in **Concepts Potentially Explained**. * Never downgrade a concept's level. If evidence is insufficient, keep the previous state. * Use prerequisite evidence: if prerequisites are not well understood, do not assign knows_well. * Do not invent concepts or evidence; rely only on the given inputs. Below is the process you should follow while analyzing and updating: 1. Check the user's previous understanding level of this concept. 2. Check the user's previous understanding level of all the prerequisites of the concept: if they are not well understood, that concept cannot be updated as well understood. 3. #15 | 2026-02-12T20:18:25.253106Z | model: gpt-5-mini | temp: 1.0 | max_tokens: 3000 | n: 1
System Prompt
You are a skilled math tutor. Your goal is to help students understand and solve problems independently. Provide guidance based on their questions or mistakes. Ask questions to encourage their thinking and let students do most of the work themselves. Never give out the solution directly to students.
User Prompt
Thanks — that explanation helped. One quick question: when deciding which piece to use for the left- or right-hand value at a boundary, is the rule always "use the piece valid for x just less than the boundary for the left-hand limit, and the piece valid for x just greater than the boundary for the right-hand limit"? Just want to be sure I apply that correctly on future piecewise problems.
Output
Yes — that rule is exactly right and is the general way to handle piecewise functions at boundary points. More precisely: - The left-hand limit at a point x = c uses the formula that applies for x values slightly less than c (the piece covering values just to the left of c). - The right-hand limit at x = c uses the formula that applies for x values slightly greater than c (the piece covering values just to the right of c). - If the function is defined at c, its actual value f(c) may come from one of the pieces (depending on how the piecewise definition specifies the intervals). For continuity you need left-hand limit = right-hand limit = f(c). A couple of quick reminders: - Pay attention to whether the interval endpoints in the piecewise definition are included (≤ or ≥) or excluded (< or >). That tells you which piece gives f(c) itself. - Even if f(c) is defined by only one piece, both one-sided limits must still match that value for continuity. - If one-sided limits differ, the function has a jump discontinuity at c. If you want, I can give a short example with different endpoint inclusions so you can practice identifying which piece to use for left, right, and f(c). Which would you prefer?

User Prompt
Thanks — that makes sense and I see how to pick the pieces for left and right limits now. Quick follow-up: if the piecewise definition uses strict inequalities on both sides (so f(c) isn't defined by either piece), we still just compare the left- and right-hand expressions — do we then say the function is discontinuous at c unless those two one-sided limits happen to be equal? Is that the right way to state it?
Output
Yes — that's exactly right. If f(c) is not defined (because neither piece includes x = c), then for continuity at c you'd need both one-sided limits to exist and be equal; only then could you extend f to be continuous there by defining f(c) equal to that common value. If the left- and right-hand limits are different (or one/both fail to exist), the function has a discontinuity at c and you cannot make it continuous at c without changing the function. More compactly: - If f(c) is defined: continuity at c requires left-hand limit = right-hand limit = f(c). - If f(c) is not defined: continuity at c would require left-hand limit = right-hand limit (and then you could define f(c) to be that common value to make it continuous). If the one-sided limits differ, the point is a jump discontinuity. Good thinking — do you want an example where f(c) isn't defined so you can practice deciding whether the limits match?

If the left- and right-hand limits are different (or one/both fail to exist), the function has a discontinuity at c and you cannot make it continuous at c without changing the function. Which function values should I compare at x = 2 and x = -2? - AI Tutor: Good — you're on the right track by thinking about the boundary points where the formula changes. Continuity at a boundary means the left-hand and right-hand limits (which for piecewise-defined simple formulas are just the function values from the appropriate pieces) agree there and equal the value of the function at that point. Identify the two boundary points: x = 2 and x = −2. 1. At x = 2: - For x > 2 the expression used is ax + 3, so the right-hand value at 2 is a·2 + 3 = 2a + 3. - For −2 ≤ x ≤ 2 the expression used at x = 2 is x − 5, so the value there is 2 − 5 = −3. Continuity at x = 2 requires the right-hand value equal the value at 2. Write that equation. 2. Just want to be sure I apply that correctly on future piecewise problems. - AI Tutor: Yes — that rule is exactly right and is the general way to handle piecewise functions at boundary points. More precisely: - The left-hand limit at a point x = c uses the formula that applies for x values slightly less than c (the piece covering values just to the left of c). - The right-hand limit at x = c uses the formula that applies for x values slightly greater than c (the piece covering values just to the right of c). - If the function is defined at c, its actual value f(c) may come from one of the pieces (depending on how the piecewise definition specifies the intervals). For continuity you need left-hand limit = right-hand limit = f(c). A couple of quick reminders: - Pay attention to whether the interval endpoints in the piecewise definition are included (≤ or ≥) or excluded (< or >). That tells you which piece gives f(c) itself. - Even if f(c) is defined by only one piece, both one-sided limits must still match that value for continuity. - If one-sided limits differ, the function has a jump discontinuity at c. Is that the right way to state it? - AI Tutor: Yes — that's exactly right. If f(c) is not defined (because neither piece includes x = c), then for continuity at c you'd need both one-sided limits to exist and be equal; only then could you extend f to be continuous there by defining f(c) equal to that common value. If the left- and right-hand limits are different (or one/both fail to exist), the function has a discontinuity at c and you cannot make it continuous at c without changing the function. If the one-sided limits differ, the point is a jump discontinuity. You are a skilled math tutor. Your goal is to help students understand and solve problems independently. Provide guidance based on their questions or mistakes. User Prompt
Thanks — that cleared it up. One quick thing I'd like to confirm: when checking continuity at a boundary I should first note which inequalities include the endpoint (≤ or ≥) to identify f(c) if it’s defined, then independently pick the piece for x<c for the left-hand limit and the piece for x>c for the right-hand limit, and finally compare left, right, and f(c) (if present). Is that the complete checklist I should use every time?
Output
Yes — that checklist is complete and a reliable procedure to follow every time. In summary, when checking continuity at a boundary point c: 1. Check whether f(c) is defined by looking at the interval endpoints (≤ / ≥ vs < / >). If included, determine f(c) from the piece that contains c. 2. For the left-hand limit, use the formula that applies for x values just less than c (the piece covering x < c near c). 3. For the right-hand limit, use the formula that applies for x values just greater than c (the piece covering x > c near c). 4. Compare: - If f(c) is defined: require left-hand limit = right-hand limit = f(c). - If f(c) is not defined: require left-hand limit = right-hand limit in order for the function to be extendable to a continuous function at c; otherwise it's discontinuous. Extra tips: - Watch endpoint inclusions carefully — they determine which piece gives f(c). - You can compute the one-sided limits by directly substituting c into the corresponding formulas when those formulas are polynomials/linear (or otherwise continuous on their intervals). - If either one-sided limit does not exist, the function is discontinuous at c. Would you like a quick example to practice this checklist?

Your analysis should be returned as updates of the user's understanding levels, formatted as a json object. There are five possible levels of understanding for each concept: * unknown_unknown: The user does not know the existence of the concept at all. * not_introduced: The user knows that such a concept exists but has no idea about it at all. * struggling: The user is having significant difficulty understanding this concept and wants to learn the most basic questions. * partial_understanding: The user has partial grasp of the concept, can ask related questions but can still make mistakes when applying. * knows_well: The user knows the concept very well and can confidently apply it without making mistakes. **Important constraints** * Only update concepts listed in **Concepts Potentially Explained**. * Never downgrade a concept's level. If evidence is insufficient, keep the previous state. * Use prerequisite evidence: if prerequisites are not well understood, do not assign knows_well. * Do not invent concepts or evidence; rely only on the given inputs. Below is the process you should follow while analyzing and updating: 1. Check the user's previous understanding level of this concept. 2. Check the user's previous understanding level of all the prerequisites of the concept: if they are not well understood, that concept cannot be updated as well understood. 3. Read the tutor's message, consider: has the tutor explained this concept clear enough? How well can the user understand it through that explanation? 4. Read the user's message, consider: has the user demonstrated good understanding of this concept after the tutor's explanation? Or did the user express further confusion? 5. For the right-hand limit, use the formula that applies for x values just greater than c (the piece covering x > c near c). 4. Compare: - If f(c) is defined: require left-hand limit = right-hand limit = f(c). - If f(c) is not defined: require left-hand limit = right-hand limit in order for the function to be extendable to a continuous function at c; otherwise it's discontinuous. Extra tips: - Watch endpoint inclusions carefully — they determine which piece gives f(c). - You can compute the one-sided limits by directly substituting c into the corresponding formulas when those formulas are polynomials/linear (or otherwise continuous on their intervals). - If either one-sided limit does not exist, the function is discontinuous at c. More compactly: - If f(c) is defined: continuity at c requires left-hand limit = right-hand limit = f(c). - If f(c) is not defined: continuity at c would require left-hand limit = right-hand limit (and then you could define f(c) to be that common value to make it continuous). If the one-sided limits differ, the point is a jump discontinuity. Good thinking — do you want an example where f(c) isn't defined so you can practice deciding whether the limits match? - You: Thanks — that cleared it up. One quick thing I'd like to confirm: when checking continuity at a boundary I should first note which inequalities include the endpoint (≤ or ≥) to identify f(c) if it’s defined, then independently pick the piece for x<c for the left-hand limit and the piece for x>c for the right-hand limit, and finally compare left, right, and f(c) (if present). Is that the complete checklist I should use every time? - AI Tutor: Yes — that checklist is complete and a reliable procedure to follow every time. In summary, when checking continuity at a boundary point c: 1. Check whether f(c) is defined by looking at the interval endpoints (≤ / ≥ vs < / >). If included, determine f(c) from the piece that contains c. 2. For the left-hand limit, use the formula that applies for x values just less than c (the piece covering x < c near c). 3. For the right-hand limit, use the formula that applies for x values just greater than c (the piece covering x > c near c). 4. Compare: - If f(c) is defined: require left-hand limit = right-hand limit = f(c). - If f(c) is not defined: require left-hand limit = right-hand limit in order for the function to be extendable to a continuous function at c; otherwise it's discontinuous. Extra tips: - Watch endpoint inclusions carefully — they determine which piece gives f(c). - You can compute the one-sided limits by directly substituting c into the corresponding formulas when those formulas are polynomials/linear (or otherwise continuous on their intervals). - If either one-sided limit does not exist, the function is discontinuous at c. User Prompt
Thanks — that clarification helped. I see that at a domain endpoint I only need the existing one-sided limit to match f(c). One quick follow-up: if the endpoint is included in a piecewise definition but the formula there is something like a fraction that becomes undefined at c (so f(c) isn't actually defined even though the inequality includes c), do we treat that the same as "f(c) not defined" and therefore say it's discontinuous unless the one-sided limit exists and we redefine f(c)? In other words, inclusion by the interval doesn't override the function being undefined at that point — right?
Output
Right — inclusion by the interval does not override the actual formula failing to produce a value at that point. If the piece that "contains" c has a formula that is undefined at c (like a division by zero, a logarithm of a nonpositive number, etc.), then f(c) is not defined in practice, even if the interval symbol includes c. You should treat that the same as any other point where f(c) is not defined. So you proceed this way: - First check whether the formula for the piece that supposedly defines f(c) actually yields a finite value at c. If it does, that is f(c). - If the formula is undefined at c, then f(c) is not defined (unless the problem explicitly states some defined value at c). - For continuity: - If f(c) is defined, require the relevant one-sided limits equal f(c) (or both one-sided limits equal f(c) if c is an interior point). - If f(c) is not defined, continuity at c would require the one-sided limits (the ones that make sense given the domain) to exist and be equal; then you could define f(c) to be that common limit to make the function continuous there. If the one-sided limits do not agree (or don't exist), the point is a discontinuity. Example to keep in mind: Suppose a piece is defined as (x^2 − 1)/(x − 1) for x ≤ 1. The algebraic expression is undefined at x = 1, even though the interval includes 1. You should note f(1) is not defined unless the definition separately specifies a value at 1. However, the one-sided limit (from the left) of that expression as x → 1− equals 2 (because the fraction simplifies to x+1 for x ≠ 1). So you could say the left-hand limit exists and equals 2, but f(1) is undefined — the function is not continuous at 1 unless you define f(1) = 2. Would you like to try a short example where the formula is undefined at the endpoint so you can apply these steps?

One quick question: when deciding which piece to use for the left- or right-hand value at a boundary, is the rule always "use the piece valid for x just less than the boundary for the left-hand limit, and the piece valid for x just greater than the boundary for the right-hand limit"? More precisely: - The left-hand limit at a point x = c uses the formula that applies for x values slightly less than c (the piece covering values just to the left of c). - The right-hand limit at x = c uses the formula that applies for x values slightly greater than c (the piece covering values just to the right of c). - If the function is defined at c, its actual value f(c) may come from one of the pieces (depending on how the piecewise definition specifies the intervals). For continuity you need left-hand limit = right-hand limit = f(c). A couple of quick reminders: - Pay attention to whether the interval endpoints in the piecewise definition are included (≤ or ≥) or excluded (< or >). That tells you which piece gives f(c) itself. - Even if f(c) is defined by only one piece, both one-sided limits must still match that value for continuity. - If one-sided limits differ, the function has a jump discontinuity at c. Is that the right way to state it? - AI Tutor: Yes — that's exactly right. If f(c) is not defined (because neither piece includes x = c), then for continuity at c you'd need both one-sided limits to exist and be equal; only then could you extend f to be continuous there by defining f(c) equal to that common value. If the left- and right-hand limits are different (or one/both fail to exist), the function has a discontinuity at c and you cannot make it continuous at c without changing the function. More compactly: - If f(c) is defined: continuity at c requires left-hand limit = right-hand limit = f(c). - If f(c) is not defined: continuity at c would require left-hand limit = right-hand limit (and then you could define f(c) to be that common value to make it continuous). In summary, when checking continuity at a boundary point c: 1. Check whether f(c) is defined by looking at the interval endpoints (≤ / ≥ vs < / >). If included, determine f(c) from the piece that contains c. 2. For the left-hand limit, use the formula that applies for x values just less than c (the piece covering x < c near c). 3. For the right-hand limit, use the formula that applies for x values just greater than c (the piece covering x > c near c). 4. If c is an endpoint of the domain (so values only exist on one side of c), you treat the missing one-sided limit as not applicable. Continuity at c then requires that the one existing one-sided limit equals f(c). More concretely: - If the domain includes values only for x ≥ c (left endpoint), check the right-hand limit lim_{x→c+} f(x). If f(c) is defined, continuity at c means lim_{x→c+} f(x) = f(c). You are a skilled math tutor. Your goal is to help students understand and solve problems independently. Provide guidance based on their questions or mistakes. User Prompt
Thanks — that clarification really helped. One quick question: when a formula is undefined at c but the one-sided limits both exist and are equal (a removable discontinuity), is it correct that we say the original function is discontinuous at c but can be made continuous by defining f(c) equal to that common limit? And in practice, when solving for unknown constants to enforce continuity across a boundary, I can treat f(c) as “not defined” initially and instead set the left- and right-hand expressions equal to each other (ignoring f(c)) to get the equation. Is that the right procedure every time in removable-discontinuity cases?
Output
Yes — that's all correct. A few precise points to keep your understanding exact: - If the formula at c is undefined (so f(c) is not actually a value), then the function as written is discontinuous at c, even if both one-sided limits exist and are equal. That kind of discontinuity is called removable because you can "remove" it by defining f(c) to be the common limit. - In solving for unknown constants to enforce continuity at a boundary where the function currently has no defined value at c, the right procedure is exactly what you described: require the left-hand limit and right-hand limit to be equal. Concretely, pick the piece valid for x < c, evaluate its formula as x → c− (usually by direct substitution if it's a polynomial/rational expression that's defined at c), pick the piece valid for x > c and evaluate as x → c+, and set those two expressions equal. Solve for the unknown(s). After that, defining f(c) to be that common value yields a continuous function at c. - If f(c) is defined but the formula gives an indeterminate/undefined expression at c, treat f(c) as not defined (unless the problem explicitly gives a value) and follow the same approach above. - Small caution: when dealing with rational expressions that simplify to a continuous expression except at c, you can often simplify first (noting equivalence for x ≠ c) and then substitute the limit value; but remember that simplification doesn't retroactively define f(c). You still must check and (if required) specify f(c). Would you like to practice this with a short example where you solve for a constant that removes a discontinuity?

Concretely, pick the piece valid for x < c, evaluate its formula as x → c− (usually by direct substitution if it's a polynomial/rational expression that's defined at c), pick the piece valid for x > c and evaluate as x → c+, and set those two expressions equal. Solve for the unknown(s). After that, defining f(c) to be that common value yields a continuous function at c. - If f(c) is defined but the formula gives an indeterminate/undefined expression at c, treat f(c) as not defined (unless the problem explicitly gives a value) and follow the same approach above. - Small caution: when dealing with rational expressions that simplify to a continuous expression except at c, you can often simplify first (noting equivalence for x ≠ c) and then substitute the limit value; but remember that simplification doesn't retroactively define f(c). You still must check and (if required) specify f(c). Output
Good question — being careful here avoids mistakes. Short answer: either approach can work, but you must keep track of where algebraic simplifications are valid. Here's a reliable procedure and the reason behind it: 1. Identify which piece applies on each side of c (x < c and x > c). For each piece, consider the expression as defining the function for values not equal to c (i.e., in a neighborhood excluding c if necessary). 2. For computing the one-sided limits, you may simplify each piece first (cancel factors, factor polynomials, etc.) as long as you remember that simplification is only valid for x values where the canceled factor is not zero. That is fine because one-sided limits look at x approaching c but not equaling c, so simplifications that are valid for x ≠ c are allowed when evaluating the limit. - Example: if a piece is (x^2 − 1)/(x − 1) for x < 1, you can simplify to x + 1 for x ≠ 1 and then substitute x = 1 to get the left-hand limit 2. 3. If you are forming algebraic equations to enforce continuity (i.e., setting left-hand limit = right-hand limit), it is safe and often simpler to simplify each expression first (valid for x ≠ c) and then substitute c to get the limit values to equate. This is the usual, reliable method when the simplifications do not depend on unknowns making denominators zero at c. 4. Caution cases to watch for: - If simplifying introduces division by an expression that might be zero for some values of the unknown constants, you must check those parameter values separately. For example, if simplification cancels a factor that contains the unknown constant, the cancellation might be invalid for certain constant values; always ensure the canceled factor is nonzero given the unknowns' values, or handle the special cases separately. - If the original piece's formula truly fails to be defined at c (division by zero etc.), remember f(c) is still undefined unless explicitly given — simplification does not define f(c), it only gives the limit. 5. Practical rule of thumb: - Simplify each side (for x ≠ c), compute the one-sided limits by substituting c into the simplified forms, set those limits equal, and solve for unknowns. - After solving, verify that your solution doesn't make any denominator (or other restricted expression) zero in the original formulas at values of x near c (which could invalidate the simplification step). If it does, handle that special case separately. Would you like to try a quick example where simplification cancels a factor so you can practice both the usual method and checking special cases?

Short answer: either approach can work, but you must keep track of where algebraic simplifications are valid. Here's a reliable procedure and the reason behind it: 1. Identify which piece applies on each side of c (x < c and x > c). For each piece, consider the expression as defining the function for values not equal to c (i.e., in a neighborhood excluding c if necessary). 2. For computing the one-sided limits, you may simplify each piece first (cancel factors, factor polynomials, etc.) as long as you remember that simplification is only valid for x values where the canceled factor is not zero. That is fine because one-sided limits look at x approaching c but not equaling c, so simplifications that are valid for x ≠ c are allowed when evaluating the limit. - Example: if a piece is (x^2 − 1)/(x − 1) for x < 1, you can simplify to x + 1 for x ≠ 1 and then substitute x = 1 to get the left-hand limit 2. 3. If you are forming algebraic equations to enforce continuity (i.e., setting left-hand limit = right-hand limit), it is safe and often simpler to simplify each expression first (valid for x ≠ c) and then substitute c to get the limit values to equate. This is the usual, reliable method when the simplifications do not depend on unknowns making denominators zero at c. 4. Caution cases to watch for: - If simplifying introduces division by an expression that might be zero for some values of the unknown constants, you must check those parameter values separately. For example, if simplification cancels a factor that contains the unknown constant, the cancellation might be invalid for certain constant values; always ensure the canceled factor is nonzero given the unknowns' values, or handle the special cases separately. - If the original piece's formula truly fails to be defined at c (division by zero etc.), remember f(c) is still undefined unless explicitly given — simplification does not define f(c), it only gives the limit. 5. Practical rule of thumb: - Simplify each side (for x ≠ c), compute the one-sided limits by substituting c into the simplified forms, set those limits equal, and solve for unknowns. - After solving, verify that your solution doesn't make any denominator (or other restricted expression) zero in the original formulas at values of x near c (which could invalidate the simplification step). If it does, handle that special case separately. Short answer: either approach can work, but you must keep track of where algebraic simplifications are valid. Here's a reliable procedure and the reason behind it: 1. Identify which piece applies on each side of c (x < c and x > c). For each piece, consider the expression as defining the function for values not equal to c (i.e., in a neighborhood excluding c if necessary). 2. For computing the one-sided limits, you may simplify each piece first (cancel factors, factor polynomials, etc.) as long as you remember that simplification is only valid for x values where the canceled factor is not zero. That is fine because one-sided limits look at x approaching c but not equaling c, so simplifications that are valid for x ≠ c are allowed when evaluating the limit. - Example: if a piece is (x^2 − 1)/(x − 1) for x < 1, you can simplify to x + 1 for x ≠ 1 and then substitute x = 1 to get the left-hand limit 2. 3. If you are forming algebraic equations to enforce continuity (i.e., setting left-hand limit = right-hand limit), it is safe and often simpler to simplify each expression first (valid for x ≠ c) and then substitute c to get the limit values to equate. This is the usual, reliable method when the simplifications do not depend on unknowns making denominators zero at c. 4. Caution cases to watch for: - If simplifying introduces division by an expression that might be zero for some values of the unknown constants, you must check those parameter values separately. For example, if simplification cancels a factor that contains the unknown constant, the cancellation might be invalid for certain constant values; always ensure the canceled factor is nonzero given the unknowns' values, or handle the special cases separately. - If the original piece's formula truly fails to be defined at c (division by zero etc.), remember f(c) is still undefined unless explicitly given — simplification does not define f(c), it only gives the limit. 5. Practical rule of thumb: - Simplify each side (for x ≠ c), compute the one-sided limits by substituting c into the simplified forms, set those limits equal, and solve for unknowns. - After solving, verify that your solution doesn't make any denominator (or other restricted expression) zero in the original formulas at values of x near c (which could invalidate the simplification step). If f(c) is not defined (because neither piece includes x = c), then for continuity at c you'd need both one-sided limits to exist and be equal; only then could you extend f to be continuous there by defining f(c) equal to that common value. This is the usual, reliable method when the simplifications do not depend on unknowns making denominators zero at c. 4. For example, if simplification cancels a factor that contains the unknown constant, the cancellation might be invalid for certain constant values; always ensure the canceled factor is nonzero given the unknowns' values, or handle the special cases separately. - If the original piece's formula truly fails to be defined at c (division by zero etc.), remember f(c) is still undefined unless explicitly given — simplification does not define f(c), it only gives the limit. 5. Practical rule of thumb: - Simplify each side (for x ≠ c), compute the one-sided limits by substituting c into the simplified forms, set those limits equal, and solve for unknowns. - After solving, verify that your solution doesn't make any denominator (or other restricted expression) zero in the original formulas at values of x near c (which could invalidate the simplification step). If it does, handle that special case separately. Would you like to try a quick example where simplification cancels a factor so you can practice both the usual method and checking special cases? # Your Message Style ## Interaction Style - Length of User Message: The user's query/response is always around 20 words. # Task Generate your next message following these rules: 1. Good attention to detail — that's exactly the right idea, and you're mostly done with the checks. Here's a concise checklist of what to do after you solve for the parameters; follow these to be thorough and avoid subtle mistakes: 1. Substitute the found parameter values into the original piecewise formulas. 2. Check the one-sided limits near the boundary by substituting the boundary value into the simplified forms (these give the limits because simplification is valid for x ≠ c). Check the original (unsimplified) formulas for any expressions that are undefined at or near c: - Verify no denominator becomes zero for x values arbitrarily close to c on the side where that piece is used. - Verify there are no other domain issues (like logs of nonpositive numbers, square roots of negatives, etc.) for x near c. 4. Check whether f(c) is actually defined in the piecewise definition: - If the interval inclusion gives f(c) (≤ or ≥) and the substituted formula is defined at c, compute f(c) and verify it equals the common one-sided limit. - If the piece's formula is undefined at c (even if the interval includes c), then f(c) is not defined; in that case the original function is still discontinuous at c until you explicitly define f(c) equal to the common limit. In short: yes — substitute parameter values back into the original formulas and check for undefined behavior near the boundary; additionally, explicitly check f(c) (if the interval includes c) to ensure it matches the one-sided limits. Those steps together constitute the complete post-solution check.

Continuity at a boundary means the left-hand and right-hand limits (which for piecewise-defined simple formulas are just the function values from the appropriate pieces) agree there and equal the value of the function at that point. One quick question: when deciding which piece to use for the left- or right-hand value at a boundary, is the rule always "use the piece valid for x just less than the boundary for the left-hand limit, and the piece valid for x just greater than the boundary for the right-hand limit"? More precisely: - The left-hand limit at a point x = c uses the formula that applies for x values slightly less than c (the piece covering values just to the left of c). - The right-hand limit at x = c uses the formula that applies for x values slightly greater than c (the piece covering values just to the right of c). - If the function is defined at c, its actual value f(c) may come from one of the pieces (depending on how the piecewise definition specifies the intervals). For continuity you need left-hand limit = right-hand limit = f(c). That tells you which piece gives f(c) itself. - Even if f(c) is defined by only one piece, both one-sided limits must still match that value for continuity. - If one-sided limits differ, the function has a jump discontinuity at c. If you want, I can give a short example with different endpoint inclusions so you can practice identifying which piece to use for left, right, and f(c). Which would you prefer? - You: Thanks — that makes sense and I see how to pick the pieces for left and right limits now. Quick follow-up: if the piecewise definition uses strict inequalities on both sides (so f(c) isn't defined by either piece), we still just compare the left- and right-hand expressions — do we then say the function is discontinuous at c unless those two one-sided limits happen to be equal? Is that the right way to state it? - AI Tutor: Yes — that's exactly right. If f(c) is not defined (because neither piece includes x = c), then for continuity at c you'd need both one-sided limits to exist and be equal; only then could you extend f to be continuous there by defining f(c) equal to that common value. If the left- and right-hand limits are different (or one/both fail to exist), the function has a discontinuity at c and you cannot make it continuous at c without changing the function. More compactly: - If f(c) is defined: continuity at c requires left-hand limit = right-hand limit = f(c). - If f(c) is not defined: continuity at c would require left-hand limit = right-hand limit (and then you could define f(c) to be that common value to make it continuous). In summary, when checking continuity at a boundary point c: 1. Check whether f(c) is defined by looking at the interval endpoints (≤ / ≥ vs < / >). If included, determine f(c) from the piece that contains c. 2. For the left-hand limit, use the formula that applies for x values just less than c (the piece covering x < c near c). 3. For the right-hand limit, use the formula that applies for x values just greater than c (the piece covering x > c near c). 4. Extra tips: - Watch endpoint inclusions carefully — they determine which piece gives f(c). - You can compute the one-sided limits by directly substituting c into the corresponding formulas when those formulas are polynomials/linear (or otherwise continuous on their intervals). - If either one-sided limit does not exist, the function is discontinuous at c. Would you like a quick example to practice this checklist? - You: Thanks — that checklist makes sense and I see how to apply it. One question: if the boundary point is also the domain endpoint (so only one side of x exists in the domain), do I treat the absent side as "not applicable" or do I still require the existing one-sided limit to equal f(c) for continuity at that endpoint? - AI Tutor: Good question — endpoints of the domain are a special but simple case. If c is an endpoint of the domain (so values only exist on one side of c), you treat the missing one-sided limit as not applicable. Continuity at c then requires that the one existing one-sided limit equals f(c). More concretely: - If the domain includes values only for x ≥ c (left endpoint), check the right-hand limit lim_{x→c+} f(x). If f(c) is defined, continuity at c means lim_{x→c+} f(x) = f(c). (The left-hand limit lim_{x→c-} is not applicable.) - If the domain includes values only for x ≤ c (right endpoint), check the left-hand limit lim_{x→c-} f(x). Continuity at c means lim_{x→c-} f(x) = f(c). (The right-hand limit is not applicable.) In short: at a domain endpoint you only require the one relevant one-sided limit to match f(c). If f(c) is not defined, you cannot have continuity at that endpoint (unless you extend the function by defining f(c) equal to the existing one-sided limit). Would you like a quick example of checking continuity at a domain endpoint? - You: Thanks — that clarification helped. I see that at a domain endpoint I only need the existing one-sided limit to match f(c). One quick follow-up: if the endpoint is included in a piecewise definition but the formula there is something like a fraction that becomes undefined at c (so f(c) isn't actually defined even though the inequality includes c), do we treat that the same as "f(c) not defined" and therefore say it's discontinuous unless the one-sided limit exists and we redefine f(c)? In other words, inclusion by the interval doesn't override the function being undefined at that point — right? - AI Tutor: Right — inclusion by the interval does not override the actual formula failing to produce a value at that point. If the piece that "contains" c has a formula that is undefined at c (like a division by zero, a logarithm of a nonpositive number, etc.), then f(c) is not defined in practice, even if the interval symbol includes c. You should treat that the same as any other point where f(c) is not defined. So you proceed this way: - First check whether the formula for the piece that supposedly defines f(c) actually yields a finite value at c. If it does, that is f(c). - If the formula is undefined at c, then f(c) is not defined (unless the problem explicitly states some defined value at c). - For continuity: - If f(c) is defined, require the relevant one-sided limits equal f(c) (or both one-sided limits equal f(c) if c is an interior point). - If f(c) is not defined, continuity at c would require the one-sided limits (the ones that make sense given the domain) to exist and be equal; then you could define f(c) to be that common limit to make the function continuous there. If the one-sided limits do not agree (or don't exist), the point is a discontinuity. Example to keep in mind: Suppose a piece is defined as (x^2 − 1)/(x − 1) for x ≤ 1. The algebraic expression is undefined at x = 1, even though the interval includes 1. You should note f(1) is not defined unless the definition separately specifies a value at 1. However, the one-sided limit (from the left) of that expression as x → 1− equals 2 (because the fraction simplifies to x+1 for x ≠ 1). So you could say the left-hand limit exists and equals 2, but f(1) is undefined — the function is not continuous at 1 unless you define f(1) = 2. Would you like to try a short example where the formula is undefined at the endpoint so you can apply these steps? - You: Thanks — that clarification really helped. One quick question: when a formula is undefined at c but the one-sided limits both exist and are equal (a removable discontinuity), is it correct that we say the original function is discontinuous at c but can be made continuous by defining f(c) equal to that common limit? And in practice, when solving for unknown constants to enforce continuity across a boundary, I can treat f(c) as “not defined” initially and instead set the left- and right-hand expressions equal to each other (ignoring f(c)) to get the equation. Is that the right procedure every time in removable-discontinuity cases? - AI Tutor: Yes — that's all correct. A few precise points to keep your understanding exact: - If the formula at c is undefined (so f(c) is not actually a value), then the function as written is discontinuous at c, even if both one-sided limits exist and are equal. That kind of discontinuity is called removable because you can "remove" it by defining f(c) to be the common limit. - In solving for unknown constants to enforce continuity at a boundary where the function currently has no defined value at c, the right procedure is exactly what you described: require the left-hand limit and right-hand limit to be equal. Concretely, pick the piece valid for x < c, evaluate its formula as x → c− (usually by direct substitution if it's a polynomial/rational expression that's defined at c), pick the piece valid for x > c and evaluate as x → c+, and set those two expressions equal. Solve for the unknown(s). After that, defining f(c) to be that common value yields a continuous function at c. - If f(c) is defined but the formula gives an indeterminate/undefined expression at c, treat f(c) as not defined (unless the problem explicitly gives a value) and follow the same approach above. - Small caution: when dealing with rational expressions that simplify to a continuous expression except at c, you can often simplify first (noting equivalence for x ≠ c) and then substitute the limit value; but remember that simplification doesn't retroactively define f(c). You still must check and (if required) specify f(c). Would you like to practice this with a short example where you solve for a constant that removes a discontinuity? - You: Thanks — that cleared up my confusion about inclusion vs. actual definability at c. One more quick thing: when the left- and right-hand expressions are algebraically different but both simplify to the same value at c (for example one side simplifies after canceling a factor that’s zero at c), is it always safe to directly substitute c into each simplified form when forming the equation for continuity? Or should I keep the original unsimplified expressions and only simplify after equating them? Which is the more reliable step to avoid mistakes when solving for unknown constants? - AI Tutor: Good question — being careful here avoids mistakes. Short answer: either approach can work, but you must keep track of where algebraic simplifications are valid. Here's a reliable procedure and the reason behind it: 1. Identify which piece applies on each side of c (x < c and x > c). For each piece, consider the expression as defining the function for values not equal to c (i.e., in a neighborhood excluding c if necessary). 2. For computing the one-sided limits, you may simplify each piece first (cancel factors, factor polynomials, etc.) as long as you remember that simplification is only valid for x values where the canceled factor is not zero. That is fine because one-sided limits look at x approaching c but not equaling c, so simplifications that are valid for x ≠ c are allowed when evaluating the limit. - Example: if a piece is (x^2 − 1)/(x − 1) for x < 1, you can simplify to x + 1 for x ≠ 1 and then substitute x = 1 to get the left-hand limit 2. 3. If you are forming algebraic equations to enforce continuity (i.e., setting left-hand limit = right-hand limit), it is safe and often simpler to simplify each expression first (valid for x ≠ c) and then substitute c to get the limit values to equate. This is the usual, reliable method when the simplifications do not depend on unknowns making denominators zero at c. 4. Caution cases to watch for: - If simplifying introduces division by an expression that might be zero for some values of the unknown constants, you must check those parameter values separately. For example, if simplification cancels a factor that contains the unknown constant, the cancellation might be invalid for certain constant values; always ensure the canceled factor is nonzero given the unknowns' values, or handle the special cases separately. - If the original piece's formula truly fails to be defined at c (division by zero etc.), remember f(c) is still undefined unless explicitly given — simplification does not define f(c), it only gives the limit. 5. Practical rule of thumb: - Simplify each side (for x ≠ c), compute the one-sided limits by substituting c into the simplified forms, set those limits equal, and solve for unknowns. - After solving, verify that your solution doesn't make any denominator (or other restricted expression) zero in the original formulas at values of x near c (which could invalidate the simplification step). If it does, handle that special case separately. If c is an endpoint of the domain (so values only exist on one side of c), you treat the missing one-sided limit as not applicable. Continuity at c then requires that the one existing one-sided limit equals f(c). More concretely: - If the domain includes values only for x ≥ c (left endpoint), check the right-hand limit lim_{x→c+} f(x). If f(c) is defined, continuity at c means lim_{x→c+} f(x) = f(c). (The left-hand limit lim_{x→c-} is not applicable.) - If the domain includes values only for x ≤ c (right endpoint), check the left-hand limit lim_{x→c-} f(x). Continuity at c means lim_{x→c-} f(x) = f(c). (The right-hand limit is not applicable.) In short: at a domain endpoint you only require the one relevant one-sided limit to match f(c). If f(c) is not defined, you cannot have continuity at that endpoint (unless you extend the function by defining f(c) equal to the existing one-sided limit). Would you like a quick example of checking continuity at a domain endpoint? - You: Thanks — that clarification helped. I see that at a domain endpoint I only need the existing one-sided limit to match f(c). One quick follow-up: if the endpoint is included in a piecewise definition but the formula there is something like a fraction that becomes undefined at c (so f(c) isn't actually defined even though the inequality includes c), do we treat that the same as "f(c) not defined" and therefore say it's discontinuous unless the one-sided limit exists and we redefine f(c)? In other words, inclusion by the interval doesn't override the function being undefined at that point — right? - AI Tutor: Right — inclusion by the interval does not override the actual formula failing to produce a value at that point. If the piece that "contains" c has a formula that is undefined at c (like a division by zero, a logarithm of a nonpositive number, etc.), then f(c) is not defined in practice, even if the interval symbol includes c. You should treat that the same as any other point where f(c) is not defined. So you proceed this way: - First check whether the formula for the piece that supposedly defines f(c) actually yields a finite value at c. If it does, that is f(c). - If the formula is undefined at c, then f(c) is not defined (unless the problem explicitly states some defined value at c). - For continuity: - If f(c) is defined, require the relevant one-sided limits equal f(c) (or both one-sided limits equal f(c) if c is an interior point). - If f(c) is not defined, continuity at c would require the one-sided limits (the ones that make sense given the domain) to exist and be equal; then you could define f(c) to be that common limit to make the function continuous there. If the one-sided limits do not agree (or don't exist), the point is a discontinuity. Example to keep in mind: Suppose a piece is defined as (x^2 − 1)/(x − 1) for x ≤ 1. The algebraic expression is undefined at x = 1, even though the interval includes 1. You should note f(1) is not defined unless the definition separately specifies a value at 1. However, the one-sided limit (from the left) of that expression as x → 1− equals 2 (because the fraction simplifies to x+1 for x ≠ 1). So you could say the left-hand limit exists and equals 2, but f(1) is undefined — the function is not continuous at 1 unless you define f(1) = 2. Would you like to try a short example where the formula is undefined at the endpoint so you can apply these steps? - You: Thanks — that clarification really helped. One quick question: when a formula is undefined at c but the one-sided limits both exist and are equal (a removable discontinuity), is it correct that we say the original function is discontinuous at c but can be made continuous by defining f(c) equal to that common limit? And in practice, when solving for unknown constants to enforce continuity across a boundary, I can treat f(c) as “not defined” initially and instead set the left- and right-hand expressions equal to each other (ignoring f(c)) to get the equation. Is that the right procedure every time in removable-discontinuity cases? - AI Tutor: Yes — that's all correct. A few precise points to keep your understanding exact: - If the formula at c is undefined (so f(c) is not actually a value), then the function as written is discontinuous at c, even if both one-sided limits exist and are equal. That kind of discontinuity is called removable because you can "remove" it by defining f(c) to be the common limit. - In solving for unknown constants to enforce continuity at a boundary where the function currently has no defined value at c, the right procedure is exactly what you described: require the left-hand limit and right-hand limit to be equal. Concretely, pick the piece valid for x < c, evaluate its formula as x → c− (usually by direct substitution if it's a polynomial/rational expression that's defined at c), pick the piece valid for x > c and evaluate as x → c+, and set those two expressions equal. Solve for the unknown(s). After that, defining f(c) to be that common value yields a continuous function at c. - If f(c) is defined but the formula gives an indeterminate/undefined expression at c, treat f(c) as not defined (unless the problem explicitly gives a value) and follow the same approach above. - Small caution: when dealing with rational expressions that simplify to a continuous expression except at c, you can often simplify first (noting equivalence for x ≠ c) and then substitute the limit value; but remember that simplification doesn't retroactively define f(c). You still must check and (if required) specify f(c). Would you like to practice this with a short example where you solve for a constant that removes a discontinuity? - You: Thanks — that cleared up my confusion about inclusion vs. actual definability at c. One more quick thing: when the left- and right-hand expressions are algebraically different but both simplify to the same value at c (for example one side simplifies after canceling a factor that’s zero at c), is it always safe to directly substitute c into each simplified form when forming the equation for continuity? Or should I keep the original unsimplified expressions and only simplify after equating them? Which is the more reliable step to avoid mistakes when solving for unknown constants? - AI Tutor: Good question — being careful here avoids mistakes. Short answer: either approach can work, but you must keep track of where algebraic simplifications are valid. Here's a reliable procedure and the reason behind it: 1. Identify which piece applies on each side of c (x < c and x > c). For each piece, consider the expression as defining the function for values not equal to c (i.e., in a neighborhood excluding c if necessary). 2. For computing the one-sided limits, you may simplify each piece first (cancel factors, factor polynomials, etc.) as long as you remember that simplification is only valid for x values where the canceled factor is not zero. That is fine because one-sided limits look at x approaching c but not equaling c, so simplifications that are valid for x ≠ c are allowed when evaluating the limit. - Example: if a piece is (x^2 − 1)/(x − 1) for x < 1, you can simplify to x + 1 for x ≠ 1 and then substitute x = 1 to get the left-hand limit 2. 3. If you are forming algebraic equations to enforce continuity (i.e., setting left-hand limit = right-hand limit), it is safe and often simpler to simplify each expression first (valid for x ≠ c) and then substitute c to get the limit values to equate. This is the usual, reliable method when the simplifications do not depend on unknowns making denominators zero at c. 4. Caution cases to watch for: - If simplifying introduces division by an expression that might be zero for some values of the unknown constants, you must check those parameter values separately. For example, if simplification cancels a factor that contains the unknown constant, the cancellation might be invalid for certain constant values; always ensure the canceled factor is nonzero given the unknowns' values, or handle the special cases separately. - If the original piece's formula truly fails to be defined at c (division by zero etc.), remember f(c) is still undefined unless explicitly given — simplification does not define f(c), it only gives the limit. 5. Practical rule of thumb: - Simplify each side (for x ≠ c), compute the one-sided limits by substituting c into the simplified forms, set those limits equal, and solve for unknowns. - After solving, verify that your solution doesn't make any denominator (or other restricted expression) zero in the original formulas at values of x near c (which could invalidate the simplification step). If it does, handle that special case separately. Would you like to try a quick example where simplification cancels a factor so you can practice both the usual method and checking special cases? - You: Thanks — that explanation helped me learn the nuance about cancellations depending on unknown constants. In short: yes — substitute parameter values back into the original formulas and check for undefined behavior near the boundary; additionally, explicitly check f(c) (if the interval includes c) to ensure it matches the one-sided limits. Short answer: after you solve for parameters, you should do both kinds of checks: - Check the domains at x = c and for x arbitrarily close to c on each relevant side (so check denominators and other domain issues at and near the boundary). - Also flag any parameter values that invalidate simplifications because they make a factor zero (or otherwise change the algebraic validity) on a neighborhood of c, even if that zero doesn't occur exactly at c. That could change whether the limit exists or what its value is. Practical checklist to follow after solving for parameters: 1.
